[1] Chapter 1
[2] 1
[3] Miss Jane Marple sat by the window. Through the window, she gazed at the garden that she once took pride in, but which had become a thing of the past. Now, as she looked out, she frowned. She had been forbidden to do any laborious gardening work for some time. She could not bend, she could not dig, she could not plant – she was limited to some pruning work. Mr. Larkin came three times a week, and he undoubtedly gave his best effort to the garden. But his "best" was according to his own standards – not according to those of his employer. Miss Marple knew her own standards well, and she knew what she wanted to do and when, so she always took the opportunity to guide him. For this, Mr. Larkin had a particular skill – he would agree wholeheartedly and then do nothing about it.
[4] "You're right, madam. The columbine should be planted there, and the bellflowers should be planted along the wall, just as you said, and that's the first thing I'll do next week."
[5] Mr. Larkin's excuses were always well-reasoned, much like Captain George in "Three Men in a Boat" who was determined to avoid going to sea. For Captain George, the wind was always a problem – it was either blowing away from the coast or into it, either blowing an unreliable west wind or a more unpredictable east wind. With Mr. Larkin, it became the weather. The weather was too dry, too wet, too waterlogged, or there was a slight frost. Or there was something more important to do – usually to plant cabbages or kale, which he enjoyed planting in excessive quantities. Mr. Larkin's principle for managing the garden was simple and could not be changed, no matter how knowledgeable the employer was.
[6] These principles included: preparing a few cups of sweet and strong tea as an encouragement for his hard work; clearing all the fallen leaves in the autumn; and leaving some space in the summer to plant his favorite plants, such as asters and lavender. In his words, it was to "make the garden look better." He agreed wholeheartedly to spray the roses for aphids, but never got around to doing it. When asked to dig the channel deeper for planting sweet peas, his response was, "You should see the sweet peas I've grown myself! Last year's harvest was good, and I didn't do any of these fancy things."
[7] To be fair, Mr. Larkin had a soft spot for his employer and would indulge their preferences (as long as it didn't involve real hard work), but he knew that vegetables were the real necessities of life. He was happy to grow kale or cabbage, or any other leafy vegetables, but flowers were what idle ladies liked best. To express his preferences, he planted the aforementioned asters, lavender, half-hardy geraniums, and summer chrysanthemums in the garden.
[8] "I've been doing gardening work in the new houses that are being built in the district. The people there want their gardens to look nice. They really do, so they bought a lot of flowers. I brought some with me, to replace the roses that were out of season and looked a bit shabby."
[9] As Miss Marple turned her gaze from the garden, she picked up her needlework.
[10] One must face the reality: Saint Mary Mead had lost its former glory. Of course, nothing can ever be the same as it was in the past. You can complain about wars (including both World Wars), the younger generation, women going out to work, atomic bombs, or the government – but the real meaning of it all is that you are getting older. Miss Marple, who was sensitive by nature, was well aware of this. But strangely enough, she couldn't help complaining about Saint Mary Mead, because it had always been her home.
[11] The oldest core of St. Mary Mead still exists. The Blue Boar is still there, as is the church and the vicarage, and the houses from the reigns of Anne Queen and George III – Miss Marple's house is one of them. Miss Hartnell and her house are struggling; Miss Weatherby has passed away, and now her house is inhabited by a bank manager's family, who have painted the doors and windows in bright turquoise. Most of the other old houses have new occupants, but they haven't altered the appearance of the houses because they like what property developers call "classic charm". They add a bathroom inside, spend a lot of money on new plumbing, an electric cooker, and a dishwasher.
[12] However, even though the houses look much the same as before, the village streets have changed. Shops change hands and immediately become more modern. The fishmongers are almost unrecognizable, with their huge, gleaming glass display cases full of frozen fish. The butchers are more conservative – good meat is still good meat, as long as you can afford it. If you can't, you have to make do with tough, cheap meat. Mr. Barnes, the grocer, remains traditional, unchanged, and for this Miss Marple, Miss Hartnell, and the rest of the village are grateful to God. His shop has comfortable chairs by the counter, where people can sit and discuss the proper way to cut bacon and the varieties of cheese.
[13] "There are all sorts of things in the packets that I've never heard of," exclaimed Miss Hartnell. "Children should have a proper breakfast of bacon and eggs, but now they're all replaced with these fancy packets of cereal. You have to carry a basket around and find what you want – sometimes it takes a quarter of an hour to find it. And then, you discover that the packaging is usually designed badly, either too big or too small. Then you have to queue up to pay, which is the most tiring part. Of course, people in the new developments think it's great."
[14] She stopped speaking.
[15] Because usually, that's where the conversation ends. In modern terms, it would be: "Developments, period." It not only has physical buildings but also represents a trend of development.
[16] 2
[17] Miss Marple sighed in disgust as she dropped another stitch in her knitting. It wasn't the first stitch she'd dropped, she had to count them to find out. She picked up a spare needle and peered at the sweater she was knitting, using the light to help her. Her new glasses weren't much help. She was aware that modern opticians could do very little for her except provide a luxurious waiting room, modern equipment, a bright light in her eyes, and an expensive bill. Miss Marple missed the days when her eyesight was so good. From her garden seat, she could see everything that happened in St. Mary Mead, and she rarely missed anything. With the help of her birdwatching binoculars (which had proved useful), she could see - she paused, lost in thought. Miss Prothero in her summer dress walking towards the parsonage garden; and the poor Prothero Colonel - he was such a boring and unpleasant man - who had been murdered in such a dreadful way. She shook her head, then thought of the parson's young, pretty wife, Greta. Dear Greta - such a loyal friend - sent her a Christmas card every year. Her little baby, who was now a handsome young man and had a good job, an engineer, perhaps? He had always liked to take his mechanical trains apart. Behind the parsonage had been a flight of steps and a field path, where Farmer Jarvis's cows would graze, and now - now -
[18] It was developments.
[19] Why couldn't developments be built? Miss Marple asked herself indignantly. Building houses was necessary, and they were built quite well - at least she had heard that. It was all due to "planning," or whatever it was called. The fact that they were all called "alleyways" was something she couldn't understand. Alleyways, Longwood Alleys, Grandison Alleys, and other alleys. But they weren't really alleys. Miss Marple knew what alleys were like. Her uncle had been a canon at Chichester Cathedral, and she had lived in an alley with him when she was a child.
[20] It was like Miss Shelley always calling Miss Marple's cramped sitting room a "reception room." Every time Miss Marple would gently correct her, "It's a reception room, Miss Shelley." And for the young and kind-hearted Miss Shelley, although she tried very
[21] How different it all was… For example, Florence the faithful, the head of the maids in the drawing room. And then there were Amy, Clara and Alice, those sweet little maids who came from the orphanage at St. Fés, where they were "trained" before going on to higher wages elsewhere. Most of them had goiters, and Amy was clearly a simpleton. They chatted and gossiped with the other maids in the village, and often went for walks with the fishmonger's assistant, the assistant gardener at the big house, and some shop assistant at Barnes' grocer's. Miss Marple fondly recalled knitting little baby clothes for the children they were about to have. They didn't use telephones and were completely ignorant of mathematics. But on the other hand, they knew how to wash dishes and make beds. They were uneducated but had skills. It was strange, now, that it was educated girls who did the housework. Foreign students, au pairs, university students on holiday, and women like Shelly Baker who lived in the "alleys" of the new developments.
[22] Oh, of course, and Miss Knight as well. I thought of her just now because she was walking back and forth on top of Miss Marple's head, making the crystal ornaments on the mantelpiece tinkle. Clearly, Miss Knight had finished her lunch and was getting ready to go for a walk, and soon she would come to ask Miss Marple if she needed anything from the village. Whenever Miss Marple thought of Miss Knight, she would have this reaction. Clearly, dear Raymond (her nephew), was very generous, and as for Miss Knight, no one could be kinder. However, her bronchitis had made her extremely weak, and Dr. Haydock had sternly warned her that she must sleep in a room with someone at all times, but - Miss Marple did not continue to think about it. Because "anyone would do, as long as it wasn't Miss Knight," such a complaint was pointless. There are not many old ladies left to choose from now. The days of the devoted maid are gone. If you really get sick, you can spend a lot of money and go through a lot of trouble to hire a good nurse to come to your house or go to the hospital, but once the illness passes, you are still in Miss Knight's hands.
[23] Miss Maple is well aware that Miss Knight, although a bit grumpy, is otherwise very good. People like her are often very caring and have a lot of affection for their employers, making them always try to cheer them up and enjoy their company. Overall, they take care of you like you are a mildly retarded child.
[24] "But," Miss Marple murmured to herself, "I may be old, but I'm not a silly old woman."
[25] At that moment, Miss Knight came in, as usual, panting and cheerfully hopping. She was fifty-six, large-bodied, with loose flesh and yellowish hair combed neatly, a long and pointed nose behind which rested a pair of glasses, below which lay a soft-lipped mouth and a somewhat underdeveloped chin.
[26] "Well!" she exclaimed cheerfully, her voice full of joy, to lift the spirits of the elderly, "I think we've all had a nap, haven't we?"
[27] "I've been knitting," Miss Marple replied, emphasizing the "I." "And," she admitted with disgust and shame, "I dropped a needle."
[28] "Oh, dear, dear," said Miss Knight, "we'll fix it soon, won't we?"
[29] "You will," said Miss Marple. "And I, alas, cannot do it."
[30] Miss Marple's slight sarcasm was not detected. Miss Knight, as usual, was very eager to help her.
[31] "There, that's all right now, dear," she said after a while.
[32] Miss Marple could stand being called "dear" (or even "sweetheart") by the greengrocer or the newsstand girl, but she couldn't stand Miss Knight using the term, and she always got angry about it. It was something old ladies had to put up with, so she merely thanked Miss Knight politely.
[33] "Now, I'm going out for a little walk," said Miss Knight in a light-hearted tone, "I won't be long."
[34] "Please don't rush back," said Miss Marple politely and sincerely.
[35] "Oh, dear, I don't want to leave you alone for too long, you'll get bored."
[36] "I assure you, I'll be fine," Miss Marple said. "I might even (she closed her eyes) take a nap."
[37] "Very well, dear. Anything you want me to bring back for you?"
[38] Miss Marple opened her eyes and thought for a moment.
[39] "You could go to the london store and see if the curtains are ready; or you could go to Mrs. Willett's and buy another skein of blue yarn; or you could go to the drugstore and get a box of blackcurrant lozenges; or you could go to the library and exchange a book for me - but don't take anything off my list, the last one was so dreadful I couldn't even finish it. She pulled out "The Awakening of Spring."
[40] "Oh, dear me, you didn't like it? I thought you were going to love it. What a lovely story."
[41] "And, if you don't mind going a bit far, perhaps you wouldn't mind going to Harritz's and seeing if they have a mixer with a hand-operated whisk – not one that you have to shake by hand."
[42] (She knew there was no such thing in the market, but Harritz was the farthest store she could go to.)
[43] "If it's not too much trouble…" Miss Marple murmured.
[44] But Miss Knight replied sincerely, "Not at all, I'd be glad to."
[45] Miss Knight loved shopping, it was as essential to her as breathing. She could spend hours doing it, guilt-free, and still have time to return home. If she met someone she knew, she could chat away with them; she could gossip with the shopkeepers and marvel at the variety of goods in different stores. It was a pleasurable activity that she could indulge in without feeling rushed to get back home.
[46] So, Miss Knight glanced at the frail old woman resting by the window and happily set out.
[47] Miss Marple waited a few minutes, fearing that Miss Knight might come back to pick up her shopping bag, wallet, or handkerchief (she was very forgetful and always had to come back for things). She also let the recent mental fatigue subside a bit, as she had come up with so many unnecessary items for Miss Knight to buy. Then, she jumped up, put aside her needlework, and strode across the room into the front hall. She took down her summer coat from the coat hook, picked up her cane from the hat stand, and switched her indoor slippers for sturdy outdoor walking shoes. Then she went out through the side door.
[48] "It would take at least an hour and a half to do all those things," she estimated, "at least – with so many people from the estates coming to shop."
[49] Miss Marple imagined Miss Knight asking about curtains at Langdon's but finding nothing, and her predictions were always surprisingly accurate. Miss Knight would now be loudly saying, "Of course, I'm quite sure they're not ready yet, but since the elderly lady mentioned it, I thought I'd come and take a look. Those poor old girls, they don't have much to look forward to anymore. We have to indulge them, and my dear is quite charming. She's getting weaker, that's the only thing we can predict, and their intelligence is declining. Hey, that fabric is nice, and what other colors do you have?"
[50] Twenty minutes passed pleasantly. When Miss Knight finally left the store, the older shopkeeper would snort derisively and mutter, "Weak, really? I'll believe it when I see it. Miss Marple, the elderly one, is as sharp as a needle, and I'll bet she hasn't changed a bit." Miss Knight then turned her attention to a young woman wearing leggings and a canvas jacket, who was now buying a plastic shower curtain with a crab pattern.
[51] "Miss Knight reminds me of Emily Waters," Miss Marple mused, pleased that she could always connect people from the past and present through their common humanity. "Their minds are both simple. Let me think, what happened to Emily?"
[52] The conclusion was that nothing much had happened. She had almost been engaged to a curate, but after years of getting to know each other, the relationship had fizzled out. Miss Marple pushed her thoughts of her companion to the back of her mind and began to pay attention to her surroundings. She quickly crossed the garden, catching a glimpse of Mr. Lecock pruning overgrown roses, but his technique seemed better suited to hybrid tea roses. However, she didn't let this bother her, and she was determined not to let it spoil her excitement and enjoyment of her little adventure. She was always eager for a thrill. She turned right at the gate to the vicarage, then took a path through the vicarage garden, and finally exited to the right. There used to be some steps there, but now there was an iron gate, beyond which was a tarred path. The path led to an attractive bridge over a stream, on the other side of which was the development.
[53] Note:
[54] Mecosoapies is a large genus of poppies, but there is no such word in English. This is likely a misreading of a complex plant name by the old gardener.
[55] Generally, bacon comes from the side and back parts of the pig, with less fat. In America, however, it is typically taken from the belly of the pig, which is fattier. Different countries may cut the pig differently when making bacon.
[56] [3] Close refers to the surrounding area and buildings of a cathedral. It is also commonly used as a street name.
[57] [4] Refers to an unmarried girl (rarely a boy) who goes to live with another family in a foreign country for an equal period of time, helping the family with childcare or household chores. The family provides room and board and pays a fixed amount of pocket money each month.
[58] Chapter 2
[59] With the spirit of Columbus discovering a new continent, Miss Marple crossed the bridge and walked along the path. Within four minutes, she was in Aubrey Street.
[60] Of course, she had seen the development area from afar on Bash Street, with its various "alleyways" and rows of neat, sturdy houses. The rooftops were adorned with television antennas, and the doors and windows were painted in shades of blue, pink, yellow, and green. It was like a real map, which she had never been in and could never belong to. Now she stood there, witnessing the emergence of a splendid new world, which her acquaintances found strange, like a well-constructed model made by children's blocks. For Miss Marple, it was not very real.
[61] Similarly, the people living there looked unreal. There were young ladies in trousers, cold and menacing young men, and fifteen-year-old girls with budding breasts. Miss Marple couldn't help but think how decadent it all seemed. She had gone so far without anyone noticing her. She emerged from Aubrey Street into Dalton Street. She walked slowly, listening intently to snippets of conversation between women pushing prams, flirting between girls and young men, and the young toughs exchanging coded messages. Mothers stood on the steps outside their houses, calling out to their children. The children, as usual, were busy doing things that adults disapproved of. Miss Marple was pleased, for children never changed. She smiled and made a mental note of it, adding it to her list of things to appreciate.
[62] The woman looked like Kitty Edwards, and the other one with slightly darker skin resembled Mary Hopper's daughter – she would probably make a mess of her wedding like Mary Hopper did. As for the boys – the dark one looked like Edward Lick, boastful but harmless, actually a nice little boy; the fair-skinned one was Josh, another son of Mrs. Beddle. They were both good kids. But the one who looked like Greggy Miggins, I'd wager, wasn't as good. I'd bet his mother and Miggins' mother were the same kind of woman…
[63] She turned a corner and entered Walcot Street, her mood rising all the time.
[64] The new world was no different from the old. The houses looked different, the street names had changed, people's clothing and speech had changed, but people were still the same. Although the wording was slightly different, the topics of conversation remained the same.
[65] Having navigated several turns during her exploration, Miss Marple gradually lost her bearings. She stopped at the edge of a residential area. She was now on Carisbrook Street, half of which was still under construction. Standing on the second floor of a nearly completed house was a young couple, discussing the surroundings.
[66] "You have to admit, this house has a good location, Harry."
[67] "The other one is just as good."
[68] "But this one has two more rooms."
[69] "But those two rooms aren't free."
[70] "Well, I like this one."
[71] "Of course you would like this!"
[72] "Oh, don't be such a wet blanket. You know what mom said."
[73] "Your mom's mouth never stops."
[74] "Don't talk about her that way. Without her, where would I be? And she could have made things worse, she could have taken you to court."
[75] "Oh, please, Lily."
[76] "This is the perfect spot to enjoy the mountain views. You can almost see—" She leans out to the left, stretching her body further out the window, "almost see the reservoir."
[77] Her body continues to lean out, completely unaware that she's putting all her weight on a loose wooden board propped up against the window sill, which can't withstand the pressure and slides out, taking her with it. She cries out, trying to regain her balance.
[78] "Harry!"
[79] The young man remains still—standing a few steps behind her, then taking a step back—
[80] The girl frantically clings to the wall, saving herself.
[81] "Oh!" She breathes a sigh of relief. "I almost fell out. Why didn't you come and pull me up?"
[82] "It all happened too quickly. But, anyway, you're alright."
[83] "You always say that. I'll tell you, I almost fell just now. Look at my coat, it's all dirty."
[84] Miss Marple had already walked several steps past them, but she suddenly turned around and came back.
[85] Lily stands by the side of the road, waiting for the young man to lock the house door.
[86] Miss Marple approached the girl and whispered quickly, "If I were you, dear, I wouldn't marry this young man. You need someone you can rely on in times of danger. I'm sorry to say these things - but I think you should be warned."
[87] After saying that, she turned and left, leaving Lily standing there watching her.
[88] "Well, all done -"
[89] Her suitor approached.
[90] "What did she say to you, Lily?"
[91] Lily opened her mouth to speak, then closed it again.
[92] "A puzzling ominous warning, if you must know."
[93] She regarded him thoughtfully.
[94] As for Miss Maple, she was eager to leave the two of them and tripped over some loose stones, falling to the ground.
[95] A woman rushed out of her house in a hurry.
[96] "Oh, my goodness, how hard you fell! Are you alright?"
[97] She hugged Miss Marple tightly with her arms open wide, pulling her up.
[98] "No broken bones, I hope? Are you feeling better now? You must have been quite frightened just now."
[99] She spoke loudly, but in a friendly manner. She was a plump, square woman, around forty years old, with brown hair that was gradually turning white, blue eyes, a large mouth with full lips, and teeth that were so white they were almost frightening.
[100] "You'd better come inside and sit down for a while, rest. I'll make you a cup of tea."
[101] Miss Marple thanked her and followed her through the blue door into a small room filled with brightly patterned chairs and sofas.
[102] "We're here," the woman said, helping Miss Marple onto a padded armchair. "You just sit here quietly for a moment while I boil the kettle."
[103] She left the room, and suddenly it was very quiet. Miss Marple took a deep breath, relieved that she was unharmed but shaken by the fall. She was getting old, after all, and a fall like that could be dangerous. Fortunately, Miss Knight would never know about it, and she felt a twinge of guilt at that thought. Miss Marple carefully moved her limbs, finding no major injuries. All she wanted now was to get home safely, or perhaps have that cup of tea.
[104] Just then, the tea arrived on a tray with a small dish of four sweet biscuits.
[105] "All ready for you," the woman said, placing the tray on the small table in front of her. "I'll pour the tea for you, and I'll put in plenty of sugar."
[106] "I don't want sugar, thank you."
[107] "You must have some sugar. You know, sugar is very good for treating shock. I went to the war and worked with the first aid team overseas, and I know it very well. I put four sugar cubes in your cup and stirred it well. After drinking this, you will be fine."
[108] Miss Marple accepted her suggestion.
[109] She is such a kind person, she thought. She reminded her of someone...but who?
[110] "You have been too kind to me," she smiled.
[111] "Oh, it's nothing. I'm just a little angel of mercy. I like to help others." The door latch clicked, and she looked out the window, "That must be my husband coming home. Arthur - there's a guest at home."
[112] She left the room and went to the front hall, with Arthur following her in, looking somewhat confused. He was thin, pale, and spoke slowly.
[113] "This lady fell just now - right in front of our house, so I brought her in here naturally."
[114] "Your wife is very kind. What's your name?"
[115] "Baddock."
[116] "Mr. Buddock, I'm sorry for all the trouble I've caused your wife."
[117] "Oh, no. It's nothing to Heather. Heather is always happy to help someone."
[118] "No, I'm just taking a walk. I live in St. Mary Mead, just behind the vicarage. My name is Marple."
[119] "Wow, really?" Heather exclaimed, "You're Miss Marple! I've heard so much about you! You're the one who solves murders, right?"
[120] "Heather! You -"
[121] "Oh, I know what you mean. I didn't mean solving murder cases, but rather investigating them. Is that right?"
[122] Miss Marple murmured modestly that she had been involved in a couple of murder cases.
[123] "I heard there was a murder in this village. People at the Bingo Club were talking about it. There was one at Gossington Hall too. I wouldn't buy a house where a murder had happened. I'm sure it would be haunted."
[124] "The murder didn't happen at Gossington Hall. The body was just moved there."
[125] "They said the body was found on the hearth rug in the library?"
[126] Miss Marple nodded.
[127] "Did you hear? They're going to make a movie out of it. That's probably why Marina Greg bought Gossington Hall. She and her husband."
[128] "Marina Greg?"
[129] "Yes, and her husband. I can't remember his name - I think he's a producer or director - Jason something. But Marina Greg is adorable, isn't she? She hasn't made many movies in recent years - she's been ill, but I still think she's one of the best actresses. Have you seen her in 'Camerella', 'The Price of Love', and 'Mary, Queen of Scots'? She's not young anymore, but she's still a fantastic actress. I've always been a huge fan of hers, and I used to dream about her when I was a teenager. My most exhilarating moment ever was when Marina Greg came to unveil a plaque for the Bermuda St. John Ambulance Brigade. I was so excited, but I got sick the day of the event, and the doctor told me I couldn't go. But I wasn't going to let illness beat me, and I didn't feel too bad anyway, so I got out of bed, put some makeup on, and went. They introduced me to her, and we talked for three minutes, and she signed my autograph book. It was amazing. I'll never forget that day."
[130] Miss Marple gazed at her.
[131] "Did it make your condition worse?" she asked, looking worried.
[132] Heather Badcock laughed.
[133] "Not at all. I felt better than ever. The thing is, if you want to do something, you have to take a risk. That's just how I am."
[134] She laughed again, her laugh sharp and triumphant.
[135] Arthur Badcock nodded approvingly. "There's never anything that can stop Heather. She always finds a way."
[136] "Eleanor Wilder," Miss Maple murmured, nodding approvingly.
[137] "What did you say?" Mr. Badcock asked.
[138] "Nothing, just someone I used to know."
[139] Heather looked at her curiously.
[140] "It's just that you reminded me of her, that's all."
[141] "Oh, I hope she's a nice person."
[142] "She is indeed a very nice person," Miss Marple said slowly. "Kind, healthy, full of life."
[143] "But I suppose she has her faults, right?" Heather said with a laugh. "I have plenty of my own."
[144] "Well, Alison is always very sure of her opinions and rarely listens to others' views, and doesn't know what her actions might do to others," Miss Marple continued.
[145] "Like the time you took in that family from the farmhouse and they left with our teaspoons," Arthur said.
[146] "But Arthur, I couldn't refuse them. It would have been too heartless."
[147] "They were antique teaspoons, sir," said Mr. Baskerville sadly. "Georgian, from my mother's grandmother."
[148] "Oh, Arthur, forget about those old teaspoons. You always go on about them."
[149] "I'm afraid I'm not very good at forgetting."
[150] Miss Marple looked thoughtful as she observed him.
[151] "What's your friend up to now?" Heather asked Miss Marple with friendly curiosity.
[152] Miss Marple hesitated for a moment before saying, "Alison Wilder? Oh, she's dead."

[154] [1] The original text is Ted, which should be referred to as a teddy boy, a term used in the 1950s to describe British young men who wore tight pants, leather jackets, and pointed shoes and were fond of rock and roll music.
[155] Chapter 3
[156] 1
[157] "I'm glad to have you back," said Mrs. Bantry, "although I had a good time."
[158] Miss Marple nodded understandingly and took a cup of tea from her friend.
[159] The husband of her friend - Colonel Bantry - had died several years ago, so she had sold Gosington Hall along with a fairly large piece of land, leaving only a small house by the east gate for herself. It was a charming little house with a porch, but it was very inconveniently situated, and even the gardener had refused to live there. Mrs. Bantry had furnished it with the necessities of modern life: a newly converted kitchen; a water supply system and an electrical system brought in from the main line; and a bathroom. These had cost her quite a bit of money, but in comparison to living in Gosington Hall, it was a small price to pay. She had also retained the necessary privacy - a garden of just over a quarter of an acre, carefully planted with various trees. She explained, "I can't see what they do with Gosington Hall, so I don't have to worry about it."
[160] In recent years, she had spent a lot of time traveling and visiting her children and grandchildren all over the world, interspersed with periods of enjoying her solitude at home. Gosington Hall had changed hands a couple of times. It had been turned into a first-class hotel, but that had failed; then it had been bought by four people, who had divided it into four apartments, but that had ended in acrimony; and finally, for some unknown reason, the Health Department had bought it, but now seemed to have decided it was not wanted after all, and it was being sold again. The two friends were now discussing this transaction.
[161]"Of course, I've heard rumors," said Miss Marple.
[162]"That's only natural," said Mrs. Bantry. "There was even a rumor that Charlie Chaplin and all his children were going to live here. That would have been interesting. But not a word of truth in it, not a word. It must have been Marjorie Greg."
[163]"How charming she was!" sighed Miss Marple. "I remember those early films of hers. She and the handsome Joel Roberts in 'The Migrants', and 'Mary Queen of Scots', and 'Through the Cornfields', although that was a very sad film, but I liked it. Oh, dear, that was a long time ago."
[164]"Yes," said Mrs. Bantry. "She must be nearly forty-five, or perhaps fifty? What do you think?"

[222] This seemingly cryptic statement was clear to Lady Brett. Long ago, Mr. Inch owned two hired carriages to pick up and drop off guests at the train station, as well as to serve the local ladies, such as taking them to tea parties or occasionally bringing his daughter to attend dull social events like balls. Mr. Inch was a jolly, ruddy-faced old man who, at the age of seventy, decided it was time for his son - whom people called "Little Inch" (then forty-five) - to take over. However, considering the son was still young and unreliable, Mr. Inch continued to drive for the elderly ladies. To keep up with the times, Little Inch replaced the carriages with cars. But he was not mechanically inclined, and soon Mr. Badwell took over the business, retaining the name "Inch." Not long after, Mr. Badwell sold the business to Mr. Roberts, but the official name in the phone book remained "Inch Car Rental Service." Therefore, the elderly ladies in the area continued to say they took the "Inch" to a place, as if they were Jonah and the "Inch" was the whale.

[272] Note:
[273] [1] An allusion to a biblical story. Jonah was thrown into the sea after disobeying God's command, and a whale swallowed him. Jonah prayed to God inside the whale's belly for three days and nights, and the whale then vomited him onto land. This is a metaphor for the car carrying the women "through thorns and thistles."
[274] [2] From Homer's epic poem The Odyssey. Penelope, the wife of Odysseus, was pursued by over a hundred suitors from various parts of the kingdom while her husband was away at war. To fend off the suitors, Penelope devised a clever ruse: she promised to remarry one of them after she had finished weaving a burial shroud for her father-in-law. During the day, she would weave the shroud, and at night, she would unravel it, delaying the suitors with her seemingly endless work, until her husband finally returned.

[277] Lady Bunty stepped back a step or two and scrutinized herself in the mirror. She adjusted her hat (which she was not used to wearing) and then donned a pair of fine leather gloves before carefully closing the door and leaving her small room. She was always brimming with anticipation for the future. It had been nearly three weeks since her last conversation with Miss Marple, and Mrs. Marina Greg and her husband had arrived at Gosington Hall and settled in.
[278] There was to be a meeting later that day, attended by the main members of the committee organizing the St. John Ambulance Corps dinner. Lady Bunty was not one of them, but she had received a note from Mrs. Marina Greg inviting her to go there for tea beforehand. The note mentioned their previous meeting in California and was signed "Sincerely, Marina Greg." The note was handwritten, not typed, which pleased and honored Lady Bunty. After all, she was an aging local celebrity, and she knew her place in the pecking order of fame and fortune. Therefore, Lady Bunty was overjoyed, like a child being treated to a special outing.
[279] As she walked down the driveway, Lady Bunty looked around with her sharp eyes, committing everything to memory. The house had been through several owners since her time at Gosington Hall, but it had become even more beautiful. "What a splendid waste of money," Lady Bunty muttered to herself, nodding in satisfaction. Although the garden was not visible from the driveway, Lady Bunty was still pleased. The garden and the special plants surrounding it were ones she had loved dearly during her time at Gosington Hall. She couldn't help but think of the irises, a bittersweet mix of nostalgia and regret. Those were truly the most beautiful iris beds in the countryside, and she was proud of them.
[280] She approached the newly painted, gleaming front door and rang the bell. A cheerful Italian butler promptly answered the door and led her straight into the late Colonel Bunty's former library. As she had heard, the room had been combined with the study, making it unforgettable. The walls were lined with paneling, the floor was parquet, and there was a grand piano at one end, with a luxurious gramophone in the middle. At the other end of the room, there was a small oasis with a Persian carpet, a tea table, and a few chairs. Mrs. Marina Greg sat at the tea table, and a man leaning against the fireplace seemed to be the ugliest man Lady Bunty had ever seen.

I feel I can settle down here and enjoy the peace and happiness. This place will be my home. Home."

If lucky, he thought, Marina should not get tired of it in two to two and a half years, but it depends on the situation.
